Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002–7013 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2630">52</a></sup> with GyDB (see URLs) as a search database. Candidates with no typical protein domains (for example, GAG, INT, RT and RT) or a tandem-repeat content greater than 20% were filtered. This procedure resulted in a final set of 73,459, 74,160 and 50,402 high-confidence full-length LTR retrotransposons in the B73, Mo17 and PH207 genomes, respectively. To calculate the insertion age of each LTR retrotransposons, 5′ and 3′ LTRs of the same element were aligned with MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2634">53</a></sup>, and the distmat utility in the EMBOSS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2638">54</a></sup> software package was used to calculate the accumulated divergence ‘K’ between 5′ and 3′ LTRs. Insertion times (<i>T</i>) of the LTR retrotransposons were calculated with the formula <i>T</i> = K/2 × <i>r</i>, where <i>r</i> is the TE-specific mutation rate of 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup> per site per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2657">55</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">RNA-seq data collection and generation</h3><p>To aid in genome annotation and to perform transcriptome analysis, we generated RNA-seq data for five different tissues from B73 and Mo17: endosperm 12 d after pollination, 14-d seedlings, bracts, roots and stems harvested in the silking stage. For each sample, two independent biological replicates were generated. All fresh tissues were frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at –80 °C before processing. Total RNA of each sample was extracted with TRIzol according to the manufacturer’s instructions. RNA-seq libraries were prepared with the Illumina standard mRNA-seq library preparation kit and sequenced on the Illumina platform with paired-end sequencing strategy.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Gene annotation</h3><p>MAKER-P version 3.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2678">56</a></sup>) was used to annotate genes in the Mo17 genome, through a comprehensive strategy combining results obtained from protein-homology-based prediction, RNA-seq-based prediction and ab initio prediction. We used the same evidence that was used for the previous B73 gene annotation, with the addition of Mo17-specific RNA-seq datasets. All annotated proteins from <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Setaria italica</i>, <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, downloaded from <a href="http://gramene.org/">http://gramene.org/</a> release 48 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tello-Ruiz, M. K. et al. Gramene 2016: comparative plant genomics and pathway resources. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1133–D1140 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2705">57</a></sup>), were used for protein-homology-based prediction. 74,471 assembled transcripts from multiple Mo17 tissues, full-length transcripts from B73 Iso-seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wang, B. et al. Unveiling the complexity of the maize transcriptome by single-molecule long-read sequencing. Nat. Commun. 7, 11708 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2709">58</a></sup>, another set of 69,163 publicly available full-length cDNAs from B73 deposited in GenBank<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Soderlund, C. et al. Sequencing, mapping, and analysis of 27,455 maize full-length cDNAs. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000740 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2713">59</a></sup>, a total of 1,574,442 Trinity-assembled transcripts from 94 B73 RNA-seq experiments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Law, M. et al. Automated update, revision, and quality control of the maize genome annotations using MAKER-P improves the B73 RefGen_v3 gene models and identifies new genes. Plant Physiol. 167, 25–39 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2717">33</a></sup> and 112,963 transcripts assembled from deep sequencing of a B73 seedling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Martin, J. A. et al. A near complete snapshot of the Zea mays seedling transcriptome revealed from ultra-deep sequencing. Sci. Rep. 4, 4519 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2722">60</a></sup> were collected and included as transcript evidence. Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Keller, O., Kollmar, M., Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. A novel hybrid gene prediction method employing protein multiple sequence alignments. Bioinformatics 27, 757–763 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2726">61</a></sup> and FGENESH (see URLs) were used for ab initio prediction of gene models in the TE-masked Mo17 genome. 44,747 genes (53,021 transcripts) were identified in the Mo17 genome and are referred to as the working gene set. This working set of gene annotations was expected to contain TEs that were not masked before annotation or annotations with poor supporting evidence. We further filtered this working set according to AED scores generated in MAKER-P software, then confirmed splice sites and performed transposon screening. Finally, 38,620 high confidence genes remained and are referred to as the filter gene set.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Identification of SNPs and indels</h3><p>We identified SNPs and insertion/deletion polymorphisms (indels, length &lt;100 bp) between the B73 and Mo17 genomes with Mummer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2738">34</a></sup> as follows: (i) The Mo17 pseudochromosome sequence was mapped to its corresponding B73 pseudochromosome with nucmer with the parameters ‘-mumreference -g 1000 -c 90 -l 40’. (ii) The delta-filter was used to filter mapping noise and determine the one-to-one alignment blocks with parameters ‘-r -q’. Alignments with aligned positions in one genome that were located more than 10 Mb away in another genome were further filtered. The aligned blocks between these two genomes were identified, and blank regions on the chromosomes that might be low-similarity regions or multiple aligned regions were filtered. (iii) Show-snps was used to obtain SNPs and small indels (&lt;100 bp). B73-genome-based SNPs and indels were detected with the parameter ‘-ClrTH’, and Mo17-genome-based parameters were detected with the parameter ‘-ClqTH’. SNPs and indels shared between the Mo17 and PH207 genomes, or the B73 and PH207 genomes, were processed with the same method used for SNPs shared between the B73 and Mo17 genomes. The genome distributions of SNPs and indels between the B73 and Mo17 genomes were also determined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Identification of PAV sequences, PAV clusters and PAV genes</h3><p>PAV sequences in the B73 and Mo17 genomes were identified through a sliding-window method. To identify B73 specific sequences, we divided the B73 genome into 500-bp overlapping windows with a step size of 100 bp and then aligned each 500-bp window against the B73 and Mo17 genomes with BWA mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2750">47</a></sup> with options ‘-w 500 --M’. The sequences of windows that could not be aligned or that aligned to the Mo17 genome with a primary alignment coverage less than 25% but could be properly aligned to the B73 genome were defined as B73-specific sequences. Overlapping windows that could not be aligned were merged. Mo17- and PH207-specific sequences were identified through the same method. Most of these PAV sequences had a relatively short length (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#Fig7">5</a>), possibly as a result of our stringent calling criteria. We further merged PAV sequences that were within 100 kb of the physical coordinates to identify PAV clusters. If a merged region had more than 10% PAV sequences, we defined this region as a PAV cluster. We listed some large PAV clusters (&gt;500 kb) in both the B73 and Mo17 genomes.</p><p>For the identification of B73-, Mo17- and PH207-specific genes, the CDS of different transcripts were merged to represent a single gene, and genes with more than 75% of the CDS regions covered by PAV sequences were defined as PAV genes. We further aligned B73 resequencing reads from maize HapMap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2760">36</a></sup>) projects to the Mo17 genome and Mo17 Illumina PCR-free reads to the B73 genome with BWA mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2764">47</a></sup> to exclude potential false positives. For the B73/Mo17-specific genes above, we filtered those with more than 50% CDS regions covered by Mo17/B73 reads to obtain the final PAV genes.</p><p>Resequencing reads of 19 wild relatives, 23 landraces and 60 modern inbred lines from maize Hapmap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet. 44, 803–807 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2771">36</a></sup>) projects were aligned to the B73, Mo17 and PH207 genomes with Bwa mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2775">47</a></sup>. The mapping depth across the PAV gene regions was calculated with samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and samtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2779">62</a></sup>. If a gene had more than 90% of the coding sequences covered by resequencing reads, we defined it as trackable in that line.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Detection of gene structural variations among B73, Mo17 and PH207</h3><p>To survey gene-structure variation between B73 and Mo17 genomes, we extended the longest transcript of each B73/Mo17 gene 2 kb upstream and downstream, and then aligned it to both Mo17/B73 genomes and the sequence from the Mo17/B73 syntenic region (in which the locations in the two genomes were less than 10 Mb apart), respectively, with Bwa mem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2791">47</a></sup>. The alignments separated by less than 20 kb were merged. For the best-hit-based method, the genome-wide best hits were used to assess gene-structure variation. Genes without amino acid substitutions or with only missense mutations and/or nonframeshift indels (length = 3<i>n</i> bp) were classed as structurally conserved genes. Genes with complete CDSs but containing SNPs or indels (3 ± 1 nt) that might produce initiation codons, termination codons, premature termination, splicing-donor-site or splicing-acceptor-site mutations, and ORF frameshifts were classified as genes with large-effect mutations. The remaining genes not identified as PAV genes were classified as genes with large structural variation.</p><p>For the synteny-based method, the best hits located in the syntenic regions were used to assess gene-structure variation. Structurally conserved genes and genes with large-effect mutations were defined according to the same criteria above. The remaining genes with more than 75% CDS missed in the syntenic regions were classed as genes with syntenic information not established or were otherwise classified as genes with large structural variation. Notably, only genes and their best hits in the counterpart genome anchored in ten pseudomolecules were included in the analysis. The comparisons of PH207 genes to the B73 and Mo17 genomes were performed in the same way.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Identification of duplicated genes and gene families</h3><p>To identify gene duplications, BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2809">63</a></sup> was used to calculate pairwise similarities (<i>e</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−20</sup>), and MCscanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2818">64</a></sup> with default parameters was then used for classification.</p><p>To identify gene families, we merged annotated genes from Mo17, B73 and three other grasses from the Phytozome database (see URLs), including <i>S. bicolor</i> (33,032 genes), <i>O. sativa</i> (39,049 genes), <i>B. distachyon</i> (31,694 genes) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (27,416 genes). The longest proteins for each gene were aligned to one another. BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2837">63</a></sup> was used to calculate pairwise similarities (<i>e</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−20</sup>), and OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2847">65</a></sup> was used to identify gene families with an inflation value of 2 and percent-match cutoff of 50.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Comparative genomic analysis among B73, Mo17, PH207 and sorghum</h3><p>To perform the comparative genomic analysis, we used the Synmap pipeline (see URLs). In brief, we used last<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Kiełbasa, S. M., Wan, R., Sato, K., Horton, P. &amp; Frith, M. C. Adaptive seeds tame genomic sequence comparison. Genome Res. 21, 487–493 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2859">66</a></sup> to blast the CDS sequence, then detected syntenic blocks with DAGchainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Haas, B. J., Delcher, A. L., Wortman, J. R. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2863">67</a></sup> with options -D 20 -A 5. Quota Align<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Tang, H. et al. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 102 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2867">68</a></sup> was further used to merge adjacent syntenic blocks. The syntenic depth was set to 2:1 for maize and sorghum and 1:1 for B73 and Mo17; B73 and PH207; and Mo17 and PH207 comparisons, and the overlapped distance was set to 40 to permit overlapped syntenic regions. Fractionation bias was applied to determine subgenome organization in maize compared with sorghum. The CodeML utility in the PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Yang, Z. &amp; Yang, Z. H. PAML 4: Phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2871">69</a></sup> software package was used to calculate the Ka and Ks rates between orthologous genes. The time of divergence from the common ancestors of maize (~2.1 Ma) was inferred on the basis of the Ks of 0.025, because maize and sorghum shared a common ancestor with ~11.9 Ma (Ks ~0.139).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Transcriptome comparison between B73 and Mo17</h3><p>The RNA-seq data for the bract, root, stem, seedling and endosperm tissues from the B73 and Mo17 lines were used to perform transcriptome comparison between B73 and Mo17. The RNA-seq data were aligned to both the B73 and Mo17 genomes with Hisat2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2884">70</a></sup>). All aligned reads were used to calculate the fragments per kilobase per million (FPKM) values with Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d34387e2888">71</a></sup>. Genes with FPKM value greater than 1 for tissues of B73 (Mo17) and lower than 0.1 in corresponding tissues of Mo17 (B73) were deemed B73 (Mo17) specifically expressed genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in the Nature Research Reporting Summary linked to this article.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Code availability</h3><p>Custom codes used in this study are currently hosted in a GitHub repository at <a href="https://github.com/caulai/Mo17_genome_assembly/">https://github.com/caulai/Mo17_genome_assembly/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Data availability</h3><p>The genome assembly and gene annotation have been deposited in the NCBI database under BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA358298">PRJNA358298</a> and BioSample number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/SAMN06169745/">SAMN06169745</a>. The GenBank accession number of the above data is <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/NCVQ00000000.1/">NCVQ00000000</a>. Raw PacBio SMRT reads, Illumina data and RNA-seq data have been deposited in the NCBI SRA database under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRP111315">SRP111315</a>.</p></div></div></section>

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="DN. Biotechnology in the 1930s: the development of hybrid maize. <i>Nat. Rev. Genet.</i> <b>2</b>, 69–74 (2001).</p><p class="c-article-references__links u-hide-print"><a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="/articles/cas-redirect/1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXisVGjs7Y%253D" aria-label="View reference 7 on CAS">CAS</a> 
    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 7 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Biotechnology%20in%20the%201930s%3A%20the%20development%20of%20hybrid%20maize&amp;journal=Nat.%20Rev.%20Genet.&amp;volume=2&amp;pages=69-74&amp;publication_year=2001&amp;author=Duvick%2CDN">
                    Google Scholar</a> 
                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="AF.                     <i>Nat Genet</i> <b>50, </b>1289–1295 (2018). https://doi.org/10.1038/s41588-018-0182-0</p><p class="c-bibliographic-information__download-citation u-hide-print"><a data-test="citation-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="download article citation" data-track-label="link" data-track-external="" href="/articles/s41588-018-0182-0.ris">Download citation<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-download"></use></svg></a></p><ul class="c-bibliographic-information__list" data-test="publication-history"><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Received<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2017-08-12">12 August 2017</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Accepted<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2018-06-05">05 June 2018</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Published<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2018-07-30">30 July 2018</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item"><p>Issue Date<span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><time datetime="2018-09">September 2018</time></span></p></li><li class="c-bibliographic-information__list-item c-bibliographic-information__list-item--doi"><p><abbr title="Digital Object Identifier">DOI</abbr><span class="u-hide">: </span><span class="c-bibliographic-information__value"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1038/s41588-018-0182-0" data-track="click" data-track-action="view doi" data-track-label="link" itemprop="sameAs">https://doi.org/10.1038/s41588-018-0182-0</a></span></p></li></ul><div data-component="share-box"></div><div data-component="article-info-list"></div></div></div></div></div></section>
